   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Global Peace Index</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub">
     <div id="mw-fr-revisiontag" class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint">
      <div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic">
       <span aria-disabled="false" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-articleSearch oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-iconWidget"></span><span id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" aria-disabled="false" title="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicator-down oo-ui-indicatorElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-indicatorWidget"></span>
      </div> 
      <div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;">
       <div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" class="flaggedrevs_short_details" style="display:none">
        This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Global_Peace_Index">reviewed</a> on <i>23 December 2020</i>.

    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <p class="mw-empty-elt"> </p> 
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Measures the relative position of nations' and regions' peacefulness
      </div> 
      <p class="mw-empty-elt"> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Global_Peace_Index.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Global_Peace_Index.svg/400px-Global_Peace_Index.svg.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Global_Peace_Index.svg/600px-Global_Peace_Index.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Global_Peace_Index.svg/800px-Global_Peace_Index.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1314"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Global_Peace_Index.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Global Peace Index 2018 (countries appearing with a deeper shade of green are ranked as more peaceful, countries appearing more red are ranked as more violent)<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><b>Global Peace Index</b> (<b>GPI</b>) is a report produced by the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Economics_%26_Peace" title="Institute for Economics &amp; Peace">Institute for Economics &amp; Peace</a> (IEP) which measures the relative position of nations' and regions' peacefulness.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup> The GPI ranks 172 independent states and territories (99.7 per cent of the world's population) according to their levels of peacefulness. In the past decade, the GPI has presented trends of increased global violence and less peacefulness.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-3">[3]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The GPI is developed in consultation with an international panel of peace experts from peace institutes and <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tanks</a> with data collected and collated by the <a href="/wiki/Economist_Intelligence_Unit" title="Economist Intelligence Unit">Economist Intelligence Unit</a>. The Index was first launched in May 2009, with subsequent reports being released annually. In 2015 it ranked 165 countries, up from 121 in 2007. The study was conceived by Australian technology entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Steve_Killelea" title="Steve Killelea">Steve Killelea</a>, and is endorsed by individuals such as former UN Secretary-General <a href="/wiki/Kofi_Annan" title="Kofi Annan">Kofi Annan</a>, <a href="/wiki/14th_Dalai_Lama" title="14th Dalai Lama">the Dalai Lama</a>, archbishop <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Tutu" title="Desmond Tutu">Desmond Tutu</a>, former President of Finland and 2008 Nobel Peace Prize laureate <a href="/wiki/Martti_Ahtisaari" title="Martti Ahtisaari">Martti Ahtisaari</a>, Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Yunus" title="Muhammad Yunus">Muhammad Yunus</a>, economist <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Sachs" title="Jeffrey Sachs">Jeffrey Sachs</a>, former president of Ireland <a href="/wiki/Mary_Robinson" title="Mary Robinson">Mary Robinson</a>, former Deputy Secretary-General of the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Jan_Eliasson" title="Jan Eliasson">Jan Eliasson</a> and former United States president <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>. The updated index is released each year at events in London, Washington, DC, and at the United Nations Secretariat in New York. </p>
      <p>The 2020 GPI indicates <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> to be the most peaceful countries, and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> to be the least peaceful.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Long-term findings of the 2017 GPI include a less peaceful world over the past decade, a 2.14 per cent deterioration in the global level of peace in the past decade, growing inequality in peace between the most and least peaceful countries, a long-term reduction in the GPI Militarization domain, and a widening impact of terrorism, with historically high numbers of people killed in terrorist incidents over the past 10 years.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The main findings of the 2017 Global Peace Index are:<sup id="cite_ref-:0_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup> </p> 
      <ul>
       <li>The overall score for the 2017 GPI improved slightly this year due to gains in six of the nine geographical regions represented. More countries improved their levels of peacefulness than deteriorated: 93 compared to 68.</li> 
       <li>Peace-building activities can be highly cost-effective, providing cost savings 16 times the cost of the intervention.</li> 
       <li>The global economic impact of violence was $14.3 trillion PPP in 2016, equivalent to 12.6 per cent of global GDP, or $1,953 per person.</li> 
       <li>In Low-Peace environments, the most important factors are related to Well-Functioning Government, Low Levels of Corruption, Acceptance of the Rights of Others and Good Relations with Neighbours</li> 
       <li>Due to armed conflict in <a href="/wiki/MENA" title="MENA">MENA</a>, many related indicators such as deaths from internal conflict, number of refugees and IDPs, and organized internal conflict are at high levels.</li> 
       <li>Safety and Security improved due to many countries recording a lower homicide rate and lower levels of political terror.</li>
      </ul> 
      <div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading">
       <input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none">

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_panel">International panel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_Peace_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: International panel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The international panel for the 2016 and 2017 GPI consisted of: </p> 
      <ul>
       <li><a href="/wiki/Kevin_P._Clements" title="Kevin P. Clements">Professor Kevin P. Clements</a>, <i>Foundation Chair of Peace and Conflict Studies and Director, National Centre for Peace and Conflict Studies, University of Otago, New Zealand</i></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Sabina_Alkire" title="Sabina Alkire">Dr. Sabina Alkire</a>, <i>Director, Oxford Poverty &amp; Human Development Initiative (OPHI), University of Oxford, United Kingdom</i></li> 
       <li>Dr. Ian Anthony, <i>Research Coordinator and Director of the Programme on Arms Control, Disarmament and Non-proliferation, Stockholm International Peace Research Institute (SIPRI), Sweden</i></li> 
       <li>Ms. Isabelle Arrandon, <i>Director of Research and Deputy Director of Communications &amp; Outreach, International Crisis Group, Belgium</i></li> 
       <li>Dr. Manuela Mesa, <i>Director, Centre for Education and Peace Research (CEIPAZ) and President, Spanish Association for Peace Research (AIPAZ), Madrid, Spain</i></li> 
       <li>Mr. <a href="/wiki/Nick_Grono" title="Nick Grono">Nick Grono</a>, <i>CEO, The Freedom Fund, United Kingdom</i></li> 
       <li>Dr. Ekaterina Stepanova, <i>Head, Unit on Peace and Conflict Studies, Institute of the World Economy and International Relations (IMEMO), Russian Academy of Sciences, Russia</i></li>
      </ul> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methodology">Methodology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_Peace_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Methodology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>In assessing peacefulness, the GPI investigates the extent to which countries are involved in ongoing domestic and international conflicts and seeks to evaluate the level of harmony or discord within a nation. Ten indicators broadly assess what might be described as safety and security in society. Their assertion is that low crime rates, minimal incidences of terrorist acts and violent demonstrations, harmonious relations with neighbouring countries, a stable political scene, and a small proportion of the population being internally displaced or refugees can be suggestive of peacefulness. </p>
      <p>In 2017, 23 indicators were used to establish peacefulness scores for each country. The indicators were originally selected with the assistance of an expert panel in 2007 and are reviewed by the expert panel on an annual basis. The scores for each indicator are normalized on a scale of 1–5, whereby qualitative indicators are banded into five groupings, and quantitative ones are scored from 1–5, to the third decimal point. A table of the indicators is below.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In the table, UCDP stands for the <a href="/wiki/Uppsala_Conflict_Data_Program" title="Uppsala Conflict Data Program">Uppsala Conflict Data Program</a> maintained by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Uppsala" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Uppsala">University of Uppsala</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, EIU for The Economist Intelligence Unit, UNSCT for the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> Survey of Criminal Trends and Operations of Criminal Justice Systems, ICPS is the <a href="/wiki/International_Centre_for_Prison_Studies" class="mw-redirect" title="International Centre for Prison Studies">International Centre for Prison Studies</a> at <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King's College London">King's College London</a>, IISS for the <a href="/wiki/International_Institute_for_Strategic_Studies" title="International Institute for Strategic Studies">International Institute for Strategic Studies</a> publication <i>The Military Balance</i>, and SIPRI for the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_International_Peace_Research_Institute" title="Stockholm International Peace Research Institute">Stockholm International Peace Research Institute</a> Arms Transfers Database. </p>

        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>16 </td> 
         <td>Number of internal security officers and police per 100,000 people </td> 
         <td>UNODC CTS; EIU estimates </td> 
         <td>Total number; Civil police force distinct from national guards or local militia<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>17 </td> 
         <td>Military expenditure as a percentage of GDP </td> 
         <td>The Military Balance and IISS </td> 
         <td>Cash outlays of central or federal government to meet costs of national armed forces, as a percentage of GDP, scores from 1 to 5 based on percentages<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>18 </td> 
         <td>Number of armed-services personnel per 100,000 </td> 
         <td>The Military Balance and IISS </td> 
         <td>All full-time active armed-services personnel </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>19 </td> 
         <td>Volume of transfers of major conventional weapons as recipient (imports) per 100,000 people </td> 
         <td>SIPRI Arms Transfers Database </td> 
         <td>Imports of major conventional weapons per 100,000 people<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>20 </td> 
         <td>Volume of transfers of major conventional weapons as supplier (exports) per 100,000 people </td> 
         <td>SIPRI Arms Transfers Database </td> 
         <td>Exports of major conventional weapons per 100,000 people </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>21 </td> 
         <td>Financial contribution to UN peacekeeping missions </td> 
         <td>United Nations Committee on Contributions and IEP </td> 
         <td>percentage of countries' "outstanding payments versus their annual assessment to the budget of the current peacekeeping missions" over an average of three years, scored from 1–5 scale based on percentage of promised contributions met </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>22 </td> 
         <td>Nuclear and heavy weapons capability </td> 
         <td>The Military Balance, IISS, SIPRI, UN Register of Conventional Arms and IEP </td> 
         <td>1–5 scale based on accumulated points; 1 point per armoured vehicle and artillery pieces, 5 points per tank, 20 points per combat aircraft, 100 points per warship, 1000 points for aircraft carrier and nuclear submarine<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td>23 </td> 
         <td>Ease of access to small arms and light weapons </td> 
         <td>EIU </td> 
         <td>Qualitative scale, ranked 1 to 5 </td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>Indicators not already ranked on a 1 to 5 scale were converted by using the following formula: <span class="texhtml">x = [x - min(x)] / [max(x) - min(x)]</span>, where max(x) and min(x) are the highest and lowest values for that indicator of the countries ranked in the index. The 0 to 1 scores that resulted were then converted to the 1 to 5 scale. Individual indicators were then weighted according to the expert panel's judgment of their importance. The scores were then tabulated into two weighted sub-indices: internal peace, weighted at 60% of a country's final score, and external peace, weighted at 40% of a country's final score. "Negative Peace," defined as the absence of violence or of the fear of violence, is used as the definition of peace to create the Global Peace Index. An additional aim of the GPI database is to facilitate deeper study of the concept of positive peace, or those attitudes, institutions, and structures that drive peacefulness in society. The GPI also examines relationships between peace and reliable international measures, including democracy and transparency, education and material well-being. As such, it seeks to understand the relative importance of a range of potential determinants, or "drivers," which may influence the nurturing of peaceful societies, both internally and externally.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Statistical analysis is applied to GPI data to uncover specific conditions conducive of peace. Researchers have determined that Positive Peace, which includes the attitudes, institutions, and structures that pre-empt conflict and facilitate functional societies, is the main driver of peace. The eight pillars of positive peace are well-functioning government, sound business environment, acceptance of the rights of others, good relations with neighbours, free flow of information, high levels of human capital, low levels of corruption, and equitable distribution of resources. Well-functioning government, low levels of corruption, acceptance of the rights of others, and good relations with neighbours are more important in countries suffering from high levels of violence. Free flow of information and sound business environment become more important when a country is approaching the global average level of peacefulness, also described as the Mid-Peace level. Low levels of corruption is the only Pillar that is strongly significant across all three levels of peacefulness. This suggests it is an important transformational factor at all stages of a nation's development. </p>

      <p><b>Note:</b> The GPI's methodology is updated regularly and is improved to reflect the most up-to-date datasets. Each year's GPI report includes a detailed description of the methodology used. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_response">International response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_Peace_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: International response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The Index has received endorsements as a political project from a number of major international figures, including the former <a href="/wiki/Secretary-General_of_the_United_Nations" title="Secretary-General of the United Nations">Secretary-General of the United Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kofi_Annan" title="Kofi Annan">Kofi Annan</a>; former <a href="/wiki/President_of_Finland" title="President of Finland">President</a> of <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and 2008 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> laureate <a href="/wiki/Martti_Ahtisaari" title="Martti Ahtisaari">Martti Ahtisaari</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Dalai_Lama" title="Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a>; Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Tutu" title="Desmond Tutu">Desmond Tutu</a>; <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Yunus" title="Muhammad Yunus">Muhammad Yunus</a>; and former United States President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Endorsers_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Endorsers-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Steve_Killelea" title="Steve Killelea">Steve Killelea</a>, A.M., the Australian philanthropist who conceived the idea of the Index, argues that the Index is "a wake-up call for leaders around the globe.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Index has been widely recognized. Professor <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Sachs" title="Jeffrey Sachs">Jeffrey Sachs</a>, Director of the Earth Institute at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>, said: "The GPI continues its pioneering work in drawing the world's attention to the massive resources we are squandering in violence and conflict. The lives and money wasted in wars, incarcerations, weapons systems, weapons trade, and more could be directed to ending poverty, promoting education, and protecting the environment. The GPI will not only draw attention to these crucial issues but help us understand them and to invest productively in a more peaceful world."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following the release of the 2015 GPI, Professor Sir Lawrence Freedman of King's College in London called the Index "an extraordinarily useful body of information," declaring of its analysis: "The best indicator of future conflict is past conflict. The challenge is how we break that cycle."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i>, in publishing the first edition of the index in 2007, admitted: "The index will run into some flak." Specifically, according to <i>The Economist</i>, the weighting of military expenditure "may seem to give heart to freeloaders: countries that enjoy peace precisely because others (often the USA) care for their defence," and said the true utility of the index may lie not in its specific current rankings of countries but in how those rankings change over time, thus tracking when and how countries become more or less peaceful.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In 2012, <i>The Economist</i> suggested: "Quantifying peace is a bit like trying to describe how happiness smells." The publication admitted that the GPI has produced some "surprising results" and argued that "part of the appeal of the index is that readers can examine each of the variables in turn and think about how much weight to add to each."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Australian_National_University" title="Australian National University">Australian National University</a> says that the GPI report presents "the latest and most comprehensive global data on trends in peace, violence and war" and "provides the world's best analysis of the statistical factors associated with long-term peace, as well as economic analysis on the macroeconomic impacts of everyday violence and war on the global economy."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The GPI has been criticized for not including indicators specifically relating to violence against women and children. In 2007 <a href="/wiki/Riane_Eisler" title="Riane Eisler">Riane Eisler</a>, writing in the <i><a href="/wiki/Christian_Science_Monitor" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Science Monitor">Christian Science Monitor</a></i>, argued: "To put it mildly, this blind spot makes the index very inaccurate." She mentions a number of specific cases, including <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, where she claims 90% of women are subject to <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">genital mutilation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, where, she says, "female infanticide is still a problem," according to a 2000 <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> study.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_leaders_talking_about_the_GPI">World leaders talking about the GPI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_Peace_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: World leaders talking about the GPI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>During a Peace Forum in August 2017, Honduran President Juan Orlando Hernandez said that "receiving such high praise from an institute that once named this country the most violent in the world is extremely significant...My administration will keep fighting to protect all Honduran citizens." The President has recently launched an initiative to build a series of safe parks across Honduras and hopes to see further improvement reflected in future GPI rankings.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Malaysia ranked 29th in the 2017 GPI. The country's Communications and Multimedia Minister, Datuk Seri Salleh Said Keruak said that this ranking along with Malaysia's high place in the 2017 World Happiness Report was proof that the "government's efforts have made Malaysia a safe and prosperous country." He also admitted, "there's still much room for improvement to make Malaysia the best among the better countries and that's what we're doing now."<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After the release of the 2016 GPI, the Botswanan Office of the President released a proud statement, "in this year's Index, Botswana was ranked as 28 out of 163 countries, up 3 places from last year. This continues to place Botswana above over half of the European region countries surveyed as well as all five of the Permanent Members of the United Nations Security Council...in addition Botswana was one of only five countries, to achieve a perfect score in the domestic and international conflict domain."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Navid Hanif, Director of the United Nations Office for ECOSOC Support and Coordination said, "it's intuitive that peace is useful and peacefulness is a reward in itself, but the IEP is trying to make the conclusion more evidence-based. Now that the index covers 99% of the population, it has come a long way. The report systemically measures peacefulness and identifies the determinants of peace."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Reacting to the 2017 results of the GPI, which ranked the Philippines 138 out of 163 countries, mainly because of poor scores in societal safety and security due to President Duterte's war on drugs, Philippine Presidential spokesman Ernesto Abella countered, "We're not entirely sure where the GPI, Global Peace Index analyst... who apparently is supposed to be a local, is really coming from. Maybe there's a political slant somewhere...based on survey results, the net satisfaction of Filipino people is quite high."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Sierra Leone ranked 39th in the 2017 Global Peace Index. Former Chief of Staff and Office of National Security (ONS) adviser, Dr. Jonathan PJ Sandy, "Welcomed the 2017 Global Peace Index report released recently which ranked Sierra Leone in first position, as the most peaceful country in West Africa and third in the African continent...He observed that going by the report itself, [future] elections might be successfully held." Presidential Spokesman, Abdulai Bayraytay "said the favourable Global Peace rating of Sierra Leone would serve as an impetus for the country to do more."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_coverage">Media coverage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_Peace_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Media coverage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The Independent: <i>Global Peace Index: US Facing New Era of Instability as Middle East Sinks Further into Turmoil</i>: "An annual global peace index has concluded that US political turmoil had pushed North America into deep instability in 2016 while the Middle East sank deeper into turmoil. Despite depicting tumult across continents, the 2017 Global Peace Index said the world had overall become more peaceful in the past year when measured against a range of indicators."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>BBC: <i>Global Peace Index 2017: World 0.28% more peaceful than last year:</i> "Levels of peace around the world have improved slightly for the first time since the Syrian war began, but harmony has decreased in the US and terrorism records have increased, a Sydney-based think-tank has found."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Forbes: "The Global Peace Index, which the Institute compiles annually, paints a sombre picture: The world has become even less peaceful in 2016, continuing a decade-long trend of increased violence and strife. Published every year since 2008, the Index ranks 163 independent states and territories by their level of peacefulness."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-3">[3]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Forbes: <i>The World's Most and Least Peaceful Countries [Infographic]:</i> "The 2017 Global Peace Index has found that the world has become a slightly safer place over the past year. However, the political fallout and deep rooted division brought on by the US presidential election campaign has led to a deterioration of peace levels in North America."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Guardian: <i>Fraught White House Campaign Blamed as US Bucks Global Trend Towards Peace:</i> "The divisive nature of Donald Trump's rise to the White House has increased mistrust of the US government and means social problems are likely to become more entrenched, said the authors of the annual Global Peace Index, in which 163 countries and territories are analysed."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>HuffingtonPost: <i>Global Peace Index 2017: Donald Trump Fallout Causes North America To Plummet Down Ranking</i>: "While the world became a safer place to live overall, the 2017 Global Peace Index found disruption caused by the perception of corruption and attacks on media in the US led to its deterioration."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Washington Times: <i>U.S. Ranked the 114th Most Peaceful Nation on Earth says Annual Global Ranking:</i> "The index is produced by the Australia-based Institute for Economics and Peace, which figures that the impact of strife worldwide is $14.3 trillion. News is not all bad, though. In a nutshell, the index found that 93 nations became 'more peaceful' in the last year, 68 were 'less peaceful.'"<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Business Insider: <i>The 12 Safest Countries in the World: "</i>The think tank Institute for Economics and Peace recently published the Global Peace Index 2017, which reveals the safest — as well as the most dangerous — countries in the world. The report ranked 163 countries based on how peaceful they are. The rankings were determined by 23 factors, which included homicide rate, political terror, and deaths from internal conflict."<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Indian news websites, ZeeNews, HindustanTimes, and Jagran Josh: The three Indian news agencies described the GPI's ranking system, global peace trends, highlights from that year's GPI and India's own placement in the GPI. The Hindustan Times quoted the GPI and emphasized that "violence impacted India's economy by USD 679.80 billion in 2016, 9&nbsp;% of India's GDP, or USD 525 per person"<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Philstar, Filipino newspaper: "Among all the 163 countries, the Philippines is ranked 138. For perspective, India is ranked just one notch above, at 137. Despite this low ranking, however, it has remained relatively stable in this low rank over time a long time. Though the raw score has worsened over the previous year, the country's rank has not been far off from this rank in previous years...Though the point of view of the report deserves respect concerning societal safety, another side of the story needs more hearing internationally."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>World Economic Forum: <i>These are the Most Peaceful Countries in the World: "</i>The Global Peace Index ranks 163 countries according to their domestic and international conflicts, safety and security and degree of militarization. It found 93 had improved, while 68 had deteriorated, and overall peace levels had inched up 0.28%."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_references">Academic references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Global_Peace_Index&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Academic references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p><i>The International Journal of Press/Politics</i>: <i>"</i>Social Media and the Arab Spring: Politics comes first": This article utilized the findings of the 2010 GPI to construct a human rights index, which was used in their overall study on the use of social media in political uprisings, and in the Arab Spring context in particular.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Contemporary Security Policy</i>: "Failed states and international order: constructing a post-Westphalian world": The Global Peace Index, along with four other global indices, is used in this study's ranking of 'state failure'. "Although this index focuses primarily on trends of armed conflict and violence it is relevant to state weakness and failure as the indicators measured for the assessment of 'peace' in this context are also indicative of state capacity."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Biological Reviews</i>: "Does Infectious Disease cause Global Variation in the Frequency of Intrastate Armed Conflict and Civil War?": This study used the 2008 Global Peace Index to build what they call a 'path analysis,' in which they sought to uncover "whether infectious disease causes the emergence of a collectivist culture."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Political Research Quarterly</i>: "Measuring the Rule of Law": This article attempts to measure the rule of law, and in doing so "correlated the rule of law indices with a measure of violent crime (for 2007) included in the Global Peace Index."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Applied Energy</i>: "The Analysis of Security Cost for Different Energy Sources": This study utilized the Global Peace Index in calculating a disruption probability from geopolitical instability, with the overall aim of analysing security costs for different sources of energy.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>International Political Science Review</i>: "Measuring Effective Democracy: A Defence": In the construction of an effective democracy index (EDI), the authors built a table that includes 2008 GPI scores as a dependent variable in a regression analysis of economic development and various indices of democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Institute for Security Studies</i>: "African Futures 2050- The Next Forty Years": The African human security research institution utilized the findings of the Global Peace Index of 2010 to emphasize trends in drug crime and violence on the African continent.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Nature Communications</i>: "Global Priorities for an Effective Information Basis of Biodiversity Distributions": In their article about insufficient digital accessible information about ecosystems and biodiversity, the authors utilized the GPI to model the "effects of secure conditions" based on the index as a measure of political stability, armed conflict, and public safety levels.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Nordic Journal of Religion and Society</i>: "Why are Danes and Swedes so Irreligious": This article uses the Global Peace Index, and its very high rankings of Denmark (3rd in 2008) and Sweden (13th in 2008) to support claims that the countries' lack of religiosity can be linked to prosperous societal structures.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Food Security</i>: "Tracking phosphorus security: indicators of phosphorus vulnerability in the global <a href="/wiki/Food_system" title="Food system">food system</a>": Along with eleven other indicators, the GPI was used as a measure of political instability for the development of a utilized in the development of a phosphorus vulnerability analysis, aimed at formulating food production methods and government policy.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>World Politics</i>: "The System Worked: Global Economic Governance During the Great Recession": Drezner uses GPI measurements, particularly the fact that interstate violence and military expenditures have decreased in the years studied, to bolster an argument suggesting that the Great Recession has not led to an increase in global violence and conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Journal of Sustainable Development Studies</i>: "Insecurity and Socio-economic Development in Nigeria": This sustainable development study utilized the GPI, in conjunction with the Human Development Index and the Corruption Perception Index to track fluctuations in Nigeria's socio-economic climate and insecurity issues over the past decade.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Harvard Educational Review</i>: "Peace Education in a Violent Culture": In criticizing the United States' culture of violence, the author refers to the developed country's remarkably low ranking on the Global Peace Index as evidence of violence's impact on societal peacefulness.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>International Security</i>: "The Heart of the Matter: The Security of Women and the Security of States": In this piece, the authors use the Global Peace Index as one of three measures of state security; the GPI is specifically used as a "general measure of state peacefulness." The report concludes that higher levels of women's physical security correlates positively with the GPI.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>The Equal Rights Review</i>: "The Mental Health Gap in South Africa: A Human Rights Issue": South Africa's poor GPI ranking, among other measures is cited by the authors as part of their overall argument that the national government is not implementing promises made towards the achievement of equality, as signatories of the United Nations Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities (CRPD).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Environment, Development and Sustainability</i>: "Creating a 'Values' Chain for Sustainable Development in Developing Nations: Where Maslow meets Porter": This study uses the 'safety and security' measures of the GPI, including political instability, level of violent crime, and likelihood of violent demonstrations, for supporting an argument that renders societal safety and security necessary for sustainable development.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> </p>

       <li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.visionofhumanity.org/">Vision of Humanity – Global Peace Index Site</a></li> 
       <li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100601214002/http://www.visionofhumanity.org/gpi-data/">Interactive world map of the Global Peace Index</a></li>

       <li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ohtopten.com/top-10-safest-countries-in-the-world/">Safest Countries in the World</a></li> 
       <li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ir.com/">Integrated Research</a> Steve Killelea is the founder of technology company Integrated Research</li> 
       <li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120701142748/http://www.ucdp.uu.se/gpdatabase/search.php">Uppsala Conflict Data Program, an organized violence database</a></li> 
       <li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.theguardian.com/news/datablog/2013/jun/11/global-peace-index-2013">Global Peace Index 2013: The Full List</a></li>
